1-A PROJECT INFORMATION  
BACKGROUND 
 With various global developments in technology and manufacturing, 
Renewable Energy (RE) has become the most affordable and cheapest source 
for meeting energy requirements. In India, the government has set an ambitious 
target of reaching 500 GW of non-fossil fuel capacity by 2030. However, 
sources such as solar and wind energy are intermittent and cannot provide a 
continuous, 24/7 supply like conventional power plants. To address this 
variability, Energy Storage Systems (ESS) are essential. ESS store excess 
energy when production exceeds demand and discharges it during peak 
periods, ensuring a stable power supply. As India increases its reliance on 
renewable energy, grid stability becomes a challenge, particularly during peak 
periods when RE generation is insufficient. This requires additional power 
sources to balance the demand. ESS plays a critical role in managing these 
fluctuations, supporting grid operators in maintaining reliability and smooth 
integration of RE into the grid. Additionally, ESS connected to SPV plants can 
enhance the capacity utilization of RE transmission systems. BESS assets will 
provide the necessary flexibility in power generation, ensuring resource 
adequacy and helping to balance supply and demand. Besides, Energy Storage 
Systems have the potential to optimize the utilization of India's transmission 
network, reducing congestion and minimizing the need for extensive 
infrastructure expansion. 
 The proposed BESS will enable non solar hour connectivity, allowing stored 
daytime solar energy to be supplied to the grid after sunset. This improves 
enhances the utilization of existing transmission infrastructure, which otherwise 
remains under-used during non-solar hours. As India's reliance on RE grows, 
maintaining grid stability-especially during peak demand periods when solar 
generation drops-has become increasingly challenging. BESS provides the 
flexibility required to balance supply and demand, support frequency regulation, 
and reduce dependence on conventional peaking power sources. 
INTRODUCTION 
NTPC GREEN ENERGY Limited (NGEL) is a subsidiary company of NTPC 
Limited which is India's largest energy conglomerate with roots planted way back 
in 1975 to accelerate power development in India.

BESS AT NTPC REL KHAVDA SOLAR PLANT

NTPC REL is developing 1200 MW ISTS connected Solar PV Projects at 
Khavda, Gujarat along with other infrastructure like land, Pooling substation, 
EHV transmission system etc.  In addition to the above, a BESS of deliverable 
capacity of 1100 MW /3300 MWh is planned for storing energy during solar 
hours from Grid and dispatch during non solar hours. While solar and other 
power substation infrastructure is being developed through separate packages. 
The BESS project will be developed in four different blocks and each block shall 
be developed through a single EPC package mode. The scope of the BESS 
Package for each block shall include   EPC works for development of BESS (in 
the land aggregated by Owner through a separate package) and power 
evacuation system up to Owner's 33kV main pooling switchgear.  
The projects are proposed to be developed in single package philosophy with three 
blocks of 300 MW and one block of 200 MW as mentioned below: 
Block-1 
	NTPC 	REL 	Khavda 
Solar Plant -PSS-1 
300 MW / 900 MWh 
Block-2 
	NTPC 	REL 	Khavda 
Solar Plant -PSS-1 
300 MW/ 900 MWh 
Block-3 
	NTPC 	REL 	Khavda 
Solar Plant -PSS-1 
300 MW/ 900 MWh 
Block-4 
	NTPC 	REL 	Khavda 
Solar Plant -PSS-1 
200 MW / 600 MWh 
 
 EPC Package: EPC works for development of BESS system (in the land 
aggregated by Owner )along with power evacuation system upto Owner's 33kV 
main pooling switchgear at NTPC REL Khavda Solar Plant PSS-1 as per 
mentioned capacity which would include Design, Engineering, Supply, Erection, 
Commissioning and associated warranty of BESS system with required  AC 
discharge capacity at Point of interconnection (POI). This would also include 
Comprehensive Operation and Maintenance (O&M) works of entire system for 
15 years from the   date of successful full commissioning of project. During the 
period of 15 years, augmentation of installed capacity and its O&M to meet 
required project performance parameters is also included in scope of bidder.

TECHNOLOGY 
Battery Energy Storage Systems" or "BESS" shall be utilizing methods and 
technologies such as Advance Chemistry Cells (ACCs), the new generation 
advance energy storage technologies that can store electric energy either as 
electrochemical or as chemical energy and convert it back to electric energy as 
and when required.  
STATUTORY COMPLIANCE, GRID CONNECTIVITY AND POWER 
EVACUATION  
The proposed BESS system shall be integrated at 33 kV Switchgear of the 
existing Solar Plant. Commensurate Solar for charging of these BESS 
Projects shall be developed in later stages. Hence, the BESS should be 
designed in such a way that it is capable of being operated as Stand-alone 
as well as integrated with Solar Plant.  
The Bidder shall be responsible for ensuring statutory compliances for the 
BESS upto point of interconnection(POI). Bidder shall comply all provisions 
and its amendment(s)/Clarification(s) thereof of the following: 
(i) CERC 'Detailed Procedure for Connectivity and GNA' under the Central 
Electricity Regulatory Commission (Connectivity and General Network 
      Access to the inter-State Transmission System) Regulations, 2022" (ii) 
CEA (Technical Standards for Connectivity to Grid) Regulation,2007, 2013  
2019 along with latest amendments.

Electrical Lines) Regulations,2022

Other necessary criteria i.e Central Electricity Authority, Technical Standards for 
Connectivity to the Grid, (Amendment) Regulations, 2013 and 2019 mention 
connectivity standards applicable to the wind generating stations, generating 
stations using inverters, wind - solar photo voltaic hybrid systems and energy 
storage systems. BESS, being an inverter-based power system element, shall 
also comply to the requirements specified for other generating stations using 
inverters. The safe and reliable operation of power system is ensured by 
frequency control as well as voltage control. BESS to be implemented shall 
have provisions for Primary frequency control with a droop which can be set as 
per system requirement between the range specified for wind/solar generation 
sources (inverter-based) in the Central Electricity Authority (Technical 
Standards for Connectivity to the Grid) Regulations. 
The BESS performs regulations in one or several predefined ways (e.g. 
regulating its own output power according to the orders given by SCADA 
system) to achieve an active power balance between generation and demand to 
maintain the power system frequency within a reasonable range 
Grid Compliance Requirements: -  
a.	Bidder shall provide EMS for the BESS, capable of functioning as redundant 
Power Plant Controller (PPC) and associated independent 
equipment/accessories. Detailed control logic and setting of the PPC shall 
be in line with latest CEA (Technical Standards for Connectivity to Grid) and 
as per RLDC/SLDC requirement. The BESS plant PPC networks shall be 
PLC / DCS based and shall be suitably designed, such that PPC shall 
directly and independently be able to control/communicate the individual 
PCS (dual or multi master) and redundant Power Quality Meters (IEC 
61000-4-30 class A) at suitable location/final outgoing portion of plant/As per 
the directions of RLDC. It shall also be able to communicate in two ways 
with RLDC/SLDC on IEC 104 protocol for its various modes of control. 
Bidder shall provide and install PQM at each 33 KV BESS Feeder at owner's 
switchgear in addition to the requirements as per extant regulations and 
integrate with PPC at BESS end. PPC healthiness shall be monitored by 
SCADA.  
 
b.	The proposed BESS system shall be integrated at 33 kV Switchgear of the 
existing Solar Plant. Commensurate Solar for charging of these BESS 
Projects shall be developed in later stages. Hence, the BESS should be

Dynamic Model

3)	Following Reports also to be included along with the dynamic model: 
 
(i)	Parameters of Inverter (PCS) in .dyr file to be validated with the test 
report results from the LVRT/HVRT certification and the validation report 
to be submitted. 
(ii)	Simulation Report of plant model confirming CEA compliance for

short circuit study/Load flow study/harmonics analysis results.

Frequency plot of the plant.

at least 12 months before the schedule commissioning date of the project

# Note WRT Corrosion Protection: 
 
Concrete capping upto 500 mm considering C-5-M corrosion category is 
mandatory for all the foundation / support structures. Next 400 mm of structural 
height (above concrete) to be protected using epoxy based corrosion protection 
solution over & above HDG. 
 
However, for sub-structures, the chemical aggressiveness of sub soil & ground 
water shall be as per approved geotechnical investigation report and IS: 456; 
based on which measures against corrosion shall be adopted. Pollution from 
industries/factory /mines etc shall also be considered while deciding the 
corrosion category depending on the level of pollution to which location is 
exposed. 
 
Corrosive category (for Paints) 
 
Classification of environments for Corrosive category shall be in accordance 
with ISO12944-2, the applicable atmospheric corrosivity categories as per 
requirement as mentioned above. 
ISO12944-5 shall be used related to paint systems in combination with guidance 
for the selection of different types of protective paint system. 
  
Wherever specification allows LT electrical panels, UPS, SCADA panel, Fire 
protection panel etc. associated with Inverter/PCS station to be placed outdoor, 
the enclosure of the same should be well engineered product having proper 
ventilation system and must be protected from harsh environment & direct 
sunlight/rainfall. Radiation and absorption effects of outdoor environment must 
be considered for temperature rise calculations. The temperature rise should 
not

PERFORMANCE PARAMETER OF BESS FOR COMPLETE LIFE: - 
BESS of capacity 1100 MW/3300 MWh  is to be installed at PSS-1 of NTPC 
REL KHAVDA Solar Plant. Project is to be executed in total four blocks as 
per following. 
 
Block-1: 300 MW / 900 MWh at PSS-1 of NTPC REL KHAVDA Solar Plant 
Block-2: 300 MW / 900 MWh at PSS-1 of NTPC REL KHAVDA Solar Plant 
Block-3: 300 MW / 900 MWh at PSS-1 of NTPC REL KHAVDA Solar Plant 
Block-4: 200 MW / 600 MWh at PSS-1 of NTPC REL KHAVDA Solar Plant 
 
BESS capacity to be considered is the rated AC discharge capacity at Point of 
injection at ISTS substation. 
 
	i) 	Round Trip AC-AC Efficiency (RTE): - 
 
 The contractor shall ensure minimum monthly round trip efficiency of 80% as 
mentioned in technical specifications. Round Trip Efficiency (RTE) shall be 
measured at Point of Coupling (POC) mentioned in tender SLD and shall be 
Including auxiliary consumption of the BESS Plant. Daily/Monthly/Yearly 
Efficiency of BESS plant shall be calculated by contractor and suitably 
automatically logged in configured performance monitoring system of BESS 
EMS system. Suitable ABT/TEM class meters shall be used for such 
measurements.

 D≠0;  
 C<= Reflected value of Ebess x MDn at POC as per the losses (of equipment 
between POI and POC) mentioned in the Technical Specification

iii) BESS  Energy Availability during Discharging: - 
 
 The bidder shall maintain a minimum of 98% Energy availability during 
discharging period on annual basis over the complete life of the project. 
Energy availability of the Project shall mean the ability to execute a function 
i.e. discharging , when called upon to do so as per the schedule in operational 
window, subject to the minimum system ratings as specified herein. The 
Bidder shall make the BESS available for 1 operational cycle per day, i.e. 1 
complete charge-discharge cycles per day as per details mentioned in 
operational window. The EMS of BESS Plant should have facility to calculate 
Energy availability of the BESS. The annual availability shall commence from 
the date of commissioning and shall be calculated as below:  
 
𝐵𝐸𝑆𝑆 𝑠𝑦𝑠𝑡𝑒𝑚 𝐴𝑣𝑎𝑖𝑙𝑎𝑏𝑖𝑙𝑖𝑡𝑦 𝑑𝑢𝑟𝑖𝑛𝑔 𝐷𝑖𝑠𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔𝑖𝑛𝑔 𝑖𝑛 𝑎 𝑡𝑖𝑚𝑒 𝑏𝑙𝑜𝑐𝑘 =
𝐴𝑐𝑡𝑢𝑎𝑙 𝑖𝑛𝑗𝑒𝑐𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛/𝑑𝑖𝑠𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔𝑒 𝑀𝑈𝑖(𝐴′)
   
𝑆𝑐ℎ𝑒𝑑𝑢𝑙𝑒𝑑  𝑖𝑛𝑗𝑒𝑐𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛/𝑑𝑖𝑠𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔𝑒 𝑀𝑈𝑖(𝐵′)

timeblocks during the year in which the power has been scheduled for 
discharging the BESS  
 
Where,  
a) i refers to the ith time-block in the year where Scheduled injection for 
injecting/discharging  the BESS MUi 
≠ 0.

iv)	BESS Auxiliary consumption: - 
 
 This auxiliary consumption shall be included in energy consumed for charging 
used in calculation of RTE. 
 
v)	Operational Window: 
 Operational Window shall mean the expected hours/duration of system (capacity) 
availability on each day during the term of the Contract, excluding:  
 
 a.	Minimum BESS recovery time as specified in the technical specifications.  
 b.	Grid Outages (duly certified to this effect by the Grid Operator/Plant Owner, 
as applicable) 
 c.	Planned Maintenance Outage duly informed by the bidder to the owner with 
at least 45 days prior notice, subject to total no. of planned outage period being 
not more than 34 hours in a two-month period. Bidder has to take permission 
from EIC before planning any such Preventive maintenance. Preferably 
Preventive maintenance shall be planned in non-solar & non peak hours with 
permission of EIC 
 
  Bidder will have to comply with the Charging and Discharging Schedule as 
intimated by owner/owner's representative.

 If required, It shall be the responsibility of the bidder to make periodic 
replacements/replenishments/augmentation of system capacities (to ensure 
annual guaranteed system ratings), if and when required, for the entire 
duration of the contract. Outage time as a result of replacement shall be 
counted as an 
"Accountable BESS Outage" for the purpose of computing BESS Charging 
Availability. Bidder has to inform owner of such periodic 
replacement/replenishment/ augmentation plans well in advance (at least 45 
days before the scheduled activity) and must take prior approval from Owner 
in this regard. 
 
 Bidder shall normally be asked to schedule for 1 Cycle/day.  Cycle shall 
mean charging of the BESS up to the awarded capacity (with consideration of 
RTE and allowed degradation) followed by discharge of such stored energy. 
Charging and Discharging from the BESS shall be solely as per the schedule 
by owner/ owner's representative. 
 
 During a Day, Owner/Owner's representative shall not ask for / schedule any 
BESS capacity / Energy in excess of 1 cycle of charge and discharge of 4 
hours/3 Hours/2 Hours (as applicable in the respective project) at rated 
power. Provided that, in a cycle for charging to the rated capacity at rated 
power, 5 hours/4 Hours/ 3 Hours (as applicable in the respective project) 
is permitted which could be a single stretch or multiple stretches for achieving 
100% charging of rated MWh capacity having total cumulative time period upto 
12 hours.  
 
 Similarly, for discharge, there could be a single stretch of 4 hours/3 Hours/2 
Hours (as applicable in the respective project) or multiple sections having 
total cumulative time period up to 8 hours for achieving 100% discharging of 
rated MWh capacity subject to condition that total scheduled discharge of 
energy from BESS shall be limited to current RTE % of the energy supplied by 
Owner. Also Provided that owner/owner's representative, at its discretion, can 
split the discharge of the stored energy into one or multiple sessions, The 
discharge may also be performed at below the rated power, stretching upto 8 
Hours. It is hereby clarified that the BESS should be designed to provide a 
minimum of 4 hours/3 Hours/2 Hours (as applicable in the respective 
project) of discharging capacity at rated power. However, owner/owner's 
representative, at its sole discretion, can schedule the discharging of the 
BESS in one or multiple sessions each day, at rated power or stretching upto 8 
Hours when discharged below rated power.

 Similarly, the charging cycle may, if required, be performed in a single

Vii) Installed Capacity Augmentation : 
 
 Augmentation plan should include installation of at least 15% of Awarded 
nameplate capacity spread over the period of 15 years. The specified ratings 
are only minimum and the bidder shall install additional capacity so as to meet 
required deliverable capacity during the 15 year O&M period as mentioned in 
clause(ii). The augmentation should be spread across the years with at least 
5% of Awarded capacity in each five Year time period from COD of 1-5 years, 
6-10 Years, 11-15 years. Bidder shall submit their declaration on complying 
the minimum specified augmentation plan (as mentioned in Tender Document) 
in Attachment - 9. 
 
 The Bidder shall plan and execute augmentation proactively such that 
Minimum Dispatchable Energy never falls below the applicable MDn value at 
any time during the Contract Period. Augmentation shall be executed before 
the projected degradation curve reaches the guaranteed MDn threshold. 
 
 As during augmentation, carrying out Grid compliance study to obtain 
charging approval from Grid authorities, shall be in the scope of Bidder, hence, 
the Bidder shall plan the activities in proactive manner. The Bidder must 
ensure that the Grid compliance study for the respective augmentation is 
submitted to Grid authorities at least 12 months prior to the proposed 
augmentation requirement. 
 
2.0 LIQUIDATED   DAMAGES (LD During O&M): - 
 As per the provision of the bid document, the successful bidder also needs 
to do Operation & Maintenance of the BESS system for the for a period of 15  
years from the date of commissioning. During the O&M period, the bidder 
shall be responsible for the following activities (but not limited to the following): 
 
a)	Maintaining availability of the BESS plant 
b)	Carry out preventive maintenance as per OEM practices 
c)	Carry out breakdown maintenance. 
d)	Carry out Augmentation as per requirement

During the O&M period following parameters shall be tested every year: 
a) Outage duration (calculated) 
b)	Fall in Mega Watt (MW) and Mega Watt Hour (MWh) Capacity 
c)	The efficiency of BESS system. 
 During O&M periods, at any time, if owner is of the view that the BESS is not 
performing as per requirements of technical specifications then owner may 
ask the bidder to carry out capacity tests and other functional tests (as 
applicable). In case BESS is not able to perform/function as per technical 
specification requirements. Contractor shall be responsible for taking all 
corrective actions, including installing additional battery banks, etc. (if 
required), in order to make the BESS system fully functional as per technical 
specification requirements. 
 
	a) 	LD applicable due to shortfall of Monthly Round Trip Efficiency: - 
 
 In case measured monthly round trip efficiency of BESS   𝜂𝑚𝑒𝑎𝑠(%) is less 
than  specified/required round trip efficiency as mentioned  in technical 
specifications i.e 80% in a particular month, bidder shall be liable to pay LD for 
additional consumption in charging energy on account of fall in efficiency for 
that month, which shall be calculated as follows, 
 
	𝑳𝑫	 /𝜂𝑚𝑒𝑎𝑠 − 1/𝜂𝑠𝑝𝑒𝑐𝑖𝑓𝑖𝑒𝑑 )   
 
 
Where,  
 C=Sum Total of Actual Injection/Dis-charging Mus (including auxiliary 
consumption) in a month as mentioned in clause 1.0 (i) above 
 
𝜂𝑚𝑒𝑎𝑠 =  Measured monthly round trip efficiency as mentioned in clause 1.0 
(i) above 
𝜂𝑠𝑝𝑒𝑐𝑖𝑓𝑒𝑑 = Required/specified Round Trip efficiency as mentioned in Technical 
Specifications= 80% 
I = Input/Charging Energy cost in INR/kWh i. ₹ 3.1/ kWh 
 
 
b) LD applicable due to shortfall of BESS annual Energy Availability 
during Discharging:  
 For any particular year, where the annual BESS availability during discharging 
is less than 98%

 where, 
 X' = Guaranteed annual Availability during discharge= 98%; 
 Y'= Actual annual System Availability during discharge calculated as per Cl 
1.0.(iii). above;  
 T = Existing Maximum Ceiling of Real Time Market in INR/kWh i.e INR 10 / kWh  
NOTE: T will remain same for entire contract 
B' = Scheduled injection/discharge for the particular year in MUs 
 If calculation of Availability is for partial year instead of full year. LD shall also be 
adjusted accordingly 
 
 If the maximum permissible unavailability of 2% is reached during part of a 
year, the monthly bill raised by the bidder during O&M period, shall be paid 
after deduction of the penalty for unavailability. 
 
 c)	DSM penalties:   
 
 DSM penalties (as applicable as per the relevant regulation) due to violation 
of the charging or discharging schedule of the BESS with reasons attributable 
to the bidder, shall be on the account of the Bidder. These penalties shall be 
adjusted to the periodic O&M Bills raised by Bidder. 
 d)	LD applicable due to excess Auxiliary Consumption: - 
 
 Separate LD on account of excess auxiliary consumption is not envisaged as it 
would get reflect in shortfall in RTE (with auxiliary consumption). 
 e)	LD applicable due to shortfall in deliverable capacity at the end of O&M 
period 
The bidder shall demonstrate deliverable capacity of minimum 92% (of 
awarded capacity in MWh) as at the time of handover at the end of O&M 
period. If the deliverable capacity at the time of handover is less than 92%, 
LD amount equal to amount applicable for shortfall in deliverable capacity 
demonstrated at the end of O&M period and calculated for a period of five 
years shall be applicable. 
Liquidated damages in INR = (X" - Y") x 1.25 x T x P x 365 x 1000 x5 
 
 where,

Additional Notes:  
 
 It is pertinent to mention that Liquidated Damages as specified above are 
mutually exclusive and independent, therefore, in case of levying of liquidated 
damages against shortfall of RTE, shortfall in availability during discharging 
hours, all damages shall be applicable to the Bidder. 
 
 In case of shortfall in any/all of the performance criteria of BESS during O&M, 
recovery of LD shall be first deducted from payment towards O&M contract 
value (Upto limiting level of 25% of the Annual O&M Contract Value). The 
adjustment of LD amount shall be done in the 4th Quarter. 
 
 In case the LD recovery amount exceeds above limiting value, the balance 
amount shall be recovered through Bank Guarantees (CPBG/LCBG), as 
applicable, submitted by successful bidder. The value of amount encashed 
from above BG shall have to be replenished by EPC contractor within three 
months. 
 
 
 O&M period of part commissioned capacity: Liquidated damages for 
shortfall in dispatchable capacity during O&M period of part commissioned 
project capacity shall also be applicable on pro rata basis of part 
commissioned Capacity. 
 
 In case of the BESS failing to meet minimum 50% of the stipulated 
availability applicable for a particular O&M Year as per Clause 1.0 (iii and iv) 
above, for a cumulative period of 2 years or more during the Term of the O&M, 
the BESS shall be liable for rejection as per GCC.

	  	 	 	APPENDIX - 3B 
    
BESS LIFE CYCLE EQUIPMENT PERFORMANCE BANK GURANTEE 
(LCBG)  
O&M of the Battery system requires special expertise and BESS capabilities 
are in a very nascent stage in India. Battery cost forms a substantial portion of 
BESS cost. Batteries may require replacement, if expected performance is not 
achieved.  In order to ensure performance of BESS as per design criteria and 
for fulfilment of warranty conditions, O&M for 15 years of project is also kept 
under Bidder's scope. In view of this, provision for LCBG is made for ensuring 
declared system performance for complete life of the project.  
Since CPBG is submitted by Bidder as per provisions mentioned in 
commercial portion of Bid Documents and is valid till defect liability period 
following mechanism is provided for submission and release of LCBGs and 
submission of performance insurance for design life beyond O&M period: 
Provisions for submission and release of Life Cycle Performance Bank 
Guarantee (LCBG)

APPENDIX - 5  
Requirement of Single IBR unit Simulation model & Benchmarking report : 
Requirement of Single IBR unit Simulation model & Benchmarking report- 
a.	Single SoC (Statement of Conformity) and Evaluation Report for Type test 
report as per CEA Connectivity standard mentioning all 
Hardware/software/Firmware version 
b.	The Reactive power response of the Inverter(PCS) in case of fault condition, 
should be faster to support Grid or as per Grid operator. 
c.	Availability of high-resolution data with pre fault/post fault time and 
automatic extraction of the above data into EMS/ SCADA /PPC subsequent 
to fault avoiding manual/OEM intervention. 
d.	During LVRT and HVRT, the Inverter (PCS) shall distribute its active and 
reactive capacity in such a way that first priority will be reactive power 
support as per voltage dip. The active current and overall current shall be 
limited as per the transient rated current limit of inverter (PCS). Inverters 
(PCS) should be capable to support reactive current in case of asymmetrical 
(1 ph/2 ph) fault condition. Active and reactive power response shall be 
oscillation free. 
e.	Inverter (PCS) controller Setting facility from local as per CEA Regulation. 
The Inverter (PCS) and its PSS/e and PSCAD model should have the 
tunable parameters: FRT Voltage threshold, K factor, Proportional Gain, 
Integral Gain and Active and reactive power recovery gain during fault 
recovery duration etc as recommended by grid Operator 
f.	Time synchronization facility of Inverter (PCS) with PPC/SCADA  
g.	Single IBR Controller Setting in compliance with CEA grid connectivity 
regulation. 
h.	Single IBR model shall be prepared or get from OEM (for PSS/E and 
PSCAD) and benchmark it with the lab/factory/field test measurements 
taken during certification process. Benchmarking report shall include model 
validation against all the clauses mentioned in B1 & B2 of CEA Technical 
Standards for Connectivity to the Grid (Amendment) Regulation, 2019. 
(Bidder to refer Report of the Working Group in respect of Data Submission 
Procedure and Verification of Compliance to CEA Regulations

BESS Block Configuration details: 
 
Block-1 (300 
MW/ 900 
MWh) 
BESS Block-1 shall be connected at the Indoor 33 kV main 
pooling Switchgear#1 of 300 MW Solar Plant. The Solar Plant is 
having 1x315 MVA power Transformer and the Transformer is 
having two LV. Hence, there are two sections in the existing 33 
kV main pooling Switchgear#1, each capable of evacuating 157.5 
MVA and 
Each 33 kV LV of transformer is connected to one 33 kV 
Switchgear section.  
As indicated in technical specification, Bidder must extend both 
existing 33 kV Switchgear sections to accommodate one 33 kV, 
3150 A feeder (with cable box vertical, if required) for each 33 kV 
Bus Section.  
Each of these two feeders shall be the Point of coupling and point 
of metering for RTE calculation of the BESS Block-1. 
In addition to above, in each of these 2 no of 150 MW BESS 
pooling switchgear, being developed by Bidder, Bidder to provide  
•	One spare outgoing feeder as indicated in tender SLD. 
•	space for future extension for 2 no. panels

1-B INTENT OF SPECIFICATION 
 SCOPE OF WORK FOR BATTERY ENERGY STORAGE SYSTEM: 
 
The scope of work covers the following activities and services in respect of all 
the equipment & works specified and covered under the specifications and read 
in conjunction with "Scope of Supply & services" elaborated elsewhere in the 
technical specification. Successful Bidder shall be responsible for identifying 
and providing any and all other additional equipment, components, and 
services necessary to install a fully functional battery energy storage system.  
Design, Engineering, Supply, Packaging and Forwarding, Transportation, 
Unloading, Storage, Construction, Erection, installation, Testing, 
Commissioning of grid connected Battery Energy Storage System (BESS). The 
proposed BESS system shall be integrated at 33 kV Switchgear of the existing 
Solar Plant. Commensurate Solar for charging of these BESS Projects shall be 
developed in later stages. Hence, the BESS should be designed in such a way 
that it is capable of being operated as Stand-alone as well as integrated with 
Solar Plant. Hence, the EMS provided by bidder for each block shall be capable 
of integrating with the existing Solar Project as well as the future commensurate 
Solar Plant. 
 
Integration of above equipment with Solar Plant Infra to commission a complete 
BESS system integrated with Solar plant and grid shall be included in scope of 
bidder  
Design life of BESS including that of battery shall be of 25 years (with degraded 
capacity as per bidder's proposal considering daily single cycle operation) from 
the date of commissioning. 
 
Batteries offered shall be rated for minimum 10,000 cycles of operation. 
 
The BESS Supplier shall take into consideration following loss parameters 
beyond the termination point (Point of Coupling) and up to POI while sizing the 
nameplate capacity of the BESS:   
Head 
Loss to be considered in sizing 
Power Trafo Losses 
1% 
Transmission Line Losses 
0.25%

1-C PROVENESS  
PROVENESS CRITERIA  
The bidder/his sub-vendor(s) is required to meet the Proveness criteria and/or 
qualification requirement for critical component and bought out item as per the 
criteria stipulated below: 
Power Conversion System (PCS) 
(a)	The bidder/ sub vendor should have manufactured grid-interactive 
bidirectional PCS of cumulative installed capacity of 40 MW or higher, out of 
which PCS installation at one location at least should be of 10 MW capacity or 
higher. The reference PCS installation of 10 MW or higher capacity must have 
been in successful operation for at least six (6) months prior to the following 
reference date: 
Date of submission of proveness documents to NGEL/NTPC REL or (LOA date 
+ 3 months), whichever is earlier. 
And 
(b)	The Bidder/sub-vendor should have in-house PCS routine testing facility as 
per relevant standard of PCS application to BESS 
 
Engineering Consultant/ System Integrator: - 
If the bidder itself has not done engineering/integration of grid interactive battery 
energy storage system of cumulative installed capacity of 20 MWh or higher, 
out of which at least one project should be of 5 MWh capacity or higher, in that 
case the bidder shall employ Engineering Consultant/ system integrator who 
should have done engineering/integration of grid interactive battery energy 
storage system of cumulative installed capacity of 20 MWh or higher, out of 
which at least one project should be of 5 MWh capacity or higher. The 
reference project of 5 MWh or higher capacity must have been in successful 
operation for at least one (one) month prior to the following reference date: 
Date of submission of proveness documents to NGEL/NTPC REL or (LOA 
date + 3 months), whichever is earlier.

BESS SYSTEM 
BESS shall be transportable, containerized energy storage system based on 
commercially available electrochemical storage solutions, capable of 
receiving, storing and delivering electrical energy at specified rate(s) suitable 
for the application laid out in the specifications herein. It comprises of unit 
batteries, battery management system (BMS), auxiliaries, such as HVAC and 
fire suppression systems, ac switchgear, Control Systems etc.

3.1.2. 
 
The salient Scope of Work:- 
Initial Design and Fabrication: - 
For the initial design and fabrication of the equipment, the Contractor shall 
•	Design, fabricate, and assemble a fully functional, transportable BESS that 
meets   the requirements delineated herein. This shall include a control system 
that provides standard input/output channels and appropriate control actions for 
all required operational and protective features.

Auxiliary Power 
 
The BESS shall include an auxiliary power system to meet the auxiliary power 
requirements of the BESS plant. The auxiliary power shall be drawn from 33 
KV BESS pooling Switchgear  being developed by bidder in BESS area. Aux 
supply system for the BESS Plant shall be developed in accordance with Cl. 
No. 1.32 and 1.33 of Chapter 1-B, above. 
The auxiliary power system and/or control system design shall provide for 
whatever emergency power is necessary for an orderly system shutdown 
during abnormal conditions such as a loss of grid power.  
 
Any kind of AC supply and DC supply for BESS SCADA/EMS system will be in 
scope of bidder.

Fire Protection: - 
The Contractor shall design and install a fire protection system that conforms 
to national and local codes, good engineering practice and CEA guidelines. 
The fire protection system design and associated alarms shall take into 
account that the BESS will be unattended most of the time. If required by the 
type of fire protection system provided, the Contractor shall calculate and take 
into account the heat content of the battery  cell materials in designing an 
appropriate fire protection system. Separate fire protection systems may be 
used in the battery, PCS, and control areas. For high energy density 
technologies, the contractor shall also provide thermal runaway 
characterization of the battery storage systems. 
 
Battery Energy Storage System is prone to fire hazard, the bidder shall 
provide suitable means such as fire barrier between switchyard and BESS, as 
applicable, to avoid fire to spread from BESS to Yard equipment. The safety 
of the equipment / personnel related to BESS operations will be in the scope 
of the bidder. Owner will in no way be responsible for any loss/ damage due 
to any fire accidents.

or emergency or protective operation, description of the toxic nature of the 
substances as well as treatment for exposure to it shall be included in the

Maintenance and Repair 
The Contractor shall supply all equipment, and materials and deploy

including all maintenance required to satisfy the operation performance and 
warranty terms and conditions. 
The Contractor shall list all maintenance activities to be carried out under the

description of the item, the expected frequency (maintenance interval), the 
time required to perform the maintenance, any anticipated parts replacement,

Factory Acceptance Testing of BESS 
a)	The Contractor shall develop and submit to the Employer for its review 
and approval a comprehensive FAT plan that shall demonstrate that the 
BESS will meet the requirements of the specification. The Employer shall 
have the right to request  reasonable changes to the test plan. 
b)	Where full-scale testing of larger systems at the factory may be difficult 
or impossible due to the large system, the FAT shall be carried out at a 
subsystem or  module level and shall consist of tests of suitable % of the 
subsystems or modules  ( to be decided during detailed engineering) that 
comprise the complete BESS, to the extent possible. In the FAT plan, the 
Contractor shall clearly state what is being tested and shall fully explain any 
features or functions of the fully assembled BESS that would not be fully 
tested in the reduced-scale testing proposed. In such a case, the SAT plan 
shall further describe how the tests that could not be carried out in the factory 
will instead be carried out at the site. 
c)	After the Contractor determines that the BESS is fully operational, the 
Contractor shall conduct a FAT in which the Contractor shall demonstrate to 
the Employer  that the BESS is fully operational and performs as specified. 
This includes but is not limited to the following:

Warranty 
a)	The Contractor shall provide warranty for the entire BESS and its 
constituent equipment as per standard warranty conditions of the respective 
equipment. However, warranties for Battery containers is envisaged for 
complete life of project (15 years) as per desired operational requirement. 
 
b)	Contractor shall procure performance guarantees from the OEM to 
ensure minimum performance levels for predefined application(s) as per the 
terms of the specification. The Warranty shall clearly indicate life expectancy 
given  discharge profiles provided for the application. 
 
c)	For the battery storage (unit or racks), the warranty shall cover parts 
warranty including battery nominal capacity ratings in order to meet the 
complete project Life condton described in this specification. 
d)	Warranty replacement shall be required for individual unit batteries that 
degrade in        performance to the point at which the BESS cannot meet the 
requirementsspecified in this specification up to the End of Project Life and/or 
for unit batteries that materially degrade the availability, reliability, safety, or 
functionality of the BESS. 
e)	Additional warranty requirements are as follows: 
The warranty shall specify the terms and conditions of the warranty, including 
operating conditions requirements, procedures that must be followed, and all 
maintenance requirements. The warranty terms shall be easy to understand 
and shall be clearly stated. 
The warranty shall provide an explicit statement as to the warranted cycle life, 
warranted calendar life, warranted annual degradation and warranted round 
trip efficiency of the battery.

Scope shall include EMS configuration to operate BESS at various modes as 
per Grid requirement mentioned in specification and integrated operation with 
Solar plant and Plant Scheduling as per Grid/RLDC requirement during O&M. 
Bidder is required to provide Hybrid PPC which shall control the generation of 
Solar Plant as well BESS Plant and shall be integrated with Solar and 
optimize the BESS charging & Grid injection. The PPC shall be acting as 
hybrid PPC for controlling the entire solar capacity (of each block) and BESS 
plus any other as per employer's requirement for the project. The Hybrid PPC 
shall be capable of integrating with the master PPC of the Commensurate 
Solar Plant in future. Master PPC of commensurate solar capacity for 
charging of BESS shall be in the scope of owner. 
Corresponding capacities of Solar Plants/Blocks associated with the BESS Blocks 
are mentioned in the Appendix-6.

(Solar plus BESS) and required functionality as mentioned above shall be 
met with the Hybrid master PPC/EMS system.

Schedule Charge and Discharge: The BESS shall be provided with 
scheduled-based charging and discharging facility. The BESS plant operator 
shall be able to schedule the BESS charging and discharging power. The 
facility shall also be provided to schedule the BESS charging and discharging 
power from the Owners' customer. The BESS scheduling shall be possible for 
complete one day with 15minutes time block interval. Schedule revision shall 
be possible as many as required during operation. Schedule overwriting 
facility shall be provided. 
 
Manual Control: In this mode, the BESS shall be able to charge and 
discharge its battery through manual intervention. The BESS operator shall 
be able to provide power set point to BESS through BESS SCADA HMI. 
BESS SCADA shall also have the facility to receive the charge and discharge 
power setpoint from RLDC/LDC and owner's control center. 
Provision shall be provided to select the operation mode in the BESS SCADA HMI. 
At a time, only one operational mode shall be selected. 
 
Reactive Power Control: The BESS shall be required to provide reactive 
power (capacitive and inductive) support to the grid for voltage regulation 
purposes. BESS operator shall be able to provide reactive power/power factor 
set point through BESS SCADA HMI (manual intervention). BESS SCADA 
shall also have the facility to receive the reactive power setpoint from 
RLDC/LDC and owner's control center. 
 
Dynamic Reactive power compensation system for each Solar Block for 
compliance of dynamic reactive power compensation at rated capacity at POI 
shall be in the scope of Owner and under execution. However, Bidder can 
utilize this reactive compensation equipment for BESS Plant (corresponding to 
the designated Solar Block with which the individual BESS Block is getting 
integrated) for compliance of dynamic reactive power compensation at rated 
capacity at POI under CEA technical standard of connectivity to Grid and it's 
amendments/clarifications thereof. In addition to the dynamic reactive 
compensation system being developed by owner for the Solar Plant, if 
additional equipment is required for the BESS plant to comply with the Grid 
connectivity requirements as per Grid authorities, the same shall be in the 
scope of bidder. The BESS shall be possible to charge and discharge 
continuously at any power

Other necessary Criteria: - 
a)	BESS shall be capacity of operating in the frequency range of 47.5 Hz to 52 
Hz and be able to deliver rated output in the frequency range of 49.5 Hz to 
50.5 Hz. 
b)	BESS shall be capable of operating when voltage at the interconnection 
point onany or all phases dips/rises to the high or low levels. The levels 
applicable for wind/solar inverter-based generation may be referred as 
available in Central Electricity Authority (Technical Standards for 
Connectivity to the Grid) Regulations. 
c)	The safe and reliable operation of power system is ensured by frequency 
control    as well as voltage control. BESS to be implemented shall have 
provisions for Primary frequency control. The BESS performs regulations in 
one or several pre-defined ways (e.g. regulating its own output power 
according to the orders given by EMS/SCADA system) to achieve an active 
power balance between generation and demand to maintain the power 
system frequency within a reasonable range. 
d)	BESS shall operate to maintain voltages as per specified voltage ranges in 
Grid standards. BESS shall have feature to detect the voltage of 
interconnection point,and regulate voltage independently. The response 
time of the BESS shall not exceed the value specified in relevant standards 
or grid codes. 
e)	BESS shall operate in a manner to promote the power system reliability and 
improve the power quality. When power quality problems, such as voltage 
dip, flicker, unsatisfactory power factor, etc., occur in power system, the 
BESS

i 
 
 
 
ii 


Abstract 
In a global effort to mitigate climate change, India has shifted from coal-based to solar-based 
power generation by installing 35 GigaWatts (GW) of solar power as of June 2020. Recently, the 
government of India laid the foundation stone for the 30 GW renewable energy park in Kutch 
district, Gujarat, India. The National Thermal Power Corporation (NTPC) has been awarded a 
9500-hectare land area to install a 4.75 GW wind-solar hybrid project. The project site is close to 
the Arabian sea and in the desert. Therefore, the study on climate vulnerability is essential to 
mitigate the worst impact of flooding, rise in temperature and sea level, and cyclones. Moreover, 
water management is one of the essential factors as the site is located in an arid region. Hence, 
an analysis of climatic variables (rainfall, temperature, and wind) over the study area using 
observed data from the Indian Meteorological Department and the European Centre for Medium-
range Weather Forecasts has been carried out. In addition, the projected change in climatic 
variables using General Circulation Models under different scenarios has also been studied. The 
sea level anomaly and sea-level rise for the 25-50 years return period have been analyzed using 
Copernicus Climate Change Service and Global Tide and Surge Reanalysis data, respectively. 
High-resolution satellite images to map water extent during the recent monsoon season have 
been used. The study area's hydrological and hydraulic models to prepare the flood inundation 
maps have been set. Combining the above results, a drainage network, recharge pits, and a 
protection wall (embankment) for the NTPC site have been recommended. These solutions will 
provide smooth functioning of the solar park even under the worst conditions. 
 
1. Introduction 
Fossil fuel emits a high level of greenhouse gas and carbon dioxide, significant contributors to 
global warming, climate change, and air pollution. Moreover, climate change has put 
tremendous pressure on society and infrastructure. Therefore, the Paris climate change 
agreement was adopted by 196 countries to limit global warming. As per the agreement, India 
has agreed to cut the greenhouse gas emissions intensity of its GDP by 33-35% by 2030. To 
achieve the above target, India has decided to install 175 gigawatts (GW) of renewable energy 
plants by 2022 (NITI Aayog, 2015). 
Recently, The Government of India has come up with the world's largest renewable energy 
project to generate 30 GW of power in Kutch, Gujarat. This project helps transform 72,600 
hectares (Ha) of wasteland into a hybrid renewable energy park. For the renewable energy park 
development, land has been allotted to Adani Green Energy Ltd, Sarjan Realities Ltd, National 
Thermal Power Corporation (NTPC) Ltd, Gujarat Industries Power Company Ltd, and Gujarat 
State Electricity Corporation. The NTPC has planned to implement a solar-wind hybrid park 
with 4.75 GW power generation capacities over a 9,500 Ha land area. 
NTPC hybrid renewable energy park will develop over the arid region of Kutch, and the site is 
located 80 km from the Arabian sea towards the East. Hence, there might be flooding in the plant 
site due to tidal water and the rainwater collected from the catchment area. Therefore, a detailed 
study of the terrain, hydrology, and drainage network design is required to prevent flooding in 
the plant area. Moreover, rainwater harvesting storage structures may be necessary for the water 
management of the study area.  
2. National Thermal Power Corporation (NTPC) Ltd 
The NTPC is an Indian government undertaking working in the power sector and allied 
activities. The NTPC Ltd has commissioned 1.088 GW of renewable energy projects. Moreover, 
2.284 GW of renewable projects is under implementation. NTPC Ltd plans to establish 32 GW 
capacity plants through renewable energy by 2032. 
 
 
3. Objectives 
The main objectives of the present study are: 
•	To study existing topographical features of the proposed site and its surrounding 
area; 
•	To analyze historical and projected climatic variables over the study region; 
•	To review existing natural drainage network; 
•	To prepare flood inundation maps using satellite images and a modeling 
framework; 
•	To estimate flood hydrographs; 
•	To design a drainage network over the study region; 
•	To design rainwater harvesting storage structures and; 
•	To design a protection wall to prevent seawater from entering the site. 
 


4. Study Area 
Our study area is the NTPC plot in a 30 GW renewable energy park. NTPC plot is located 
at 24º05'N 69º30'E and has a total geographical area of 9,500 Ha (equivalent to the size of 
Rajkot city). NTPC plot is located between the India-Pakistan border and Khavda village, 
Kutch, Gujarat (Figure 1). The Khavda village is the last point accessible by the civilians in 
the area. The Border Security Force (BSF) and Army installations are beyond the India 
bridge operated by the BSF. Only the Gujarat state highway (GJ SH 45) road leads to the 
project site. Due to national security reasons, 'no-go zones' are marked around the project. 
NTPC plot is 30 km away from the India Bridge check post. Shakoor Lake (Northwest) and 
Rann of Kutch lake (East) are located near the NTPC plot. The nearest railway station and 
airport are situated in Bhuj. 
The study area is located on a tectonically active northwestern margin of the Indian 
continental shield, and it lies in seismic zone V of the seismic zoning map of India (Singh 
and Srivastava, 2011). Centuries ago, the Great Rann of Kutch was part of the Arabian sea 
(Gujarat Biodiversity Board, 2013). After the earthquake sea bed was exposed and turned 
the entire area into a desert (Das et al., 2017; Sharma et al., 2021). In 1819 a major 
earthquake destroyed the Indus river connection with Rann and Kori creek by forming the 
mound of Allah bund (Gujarat Ecology Commission, 2011; Naik et al., 2020; Singh and 
Mandal, 2020; Thakkar et al., 2012). It is a vast expanse of arid land, just 70 km from Kori 
creek. This creek is an entry point for the Arabian seawater to the Great Rann and supports 
dense patches of mangrove (Gupta et al., 2002; Kumar et al., 2017). A large part of the area 
is covered under shallow sea waters during high tides (Jothimani and Garg, 1992; Majethiya 
et al., 2016). It results in very high salt content with sodium chloride as the dominant salt 
(Central Ground Water Board, 2014; Hörmann et al., 2016; Juyal, 2014). Moreover, the 
water table of Rann ranges between 1 and 2m (ENVIS, 2012). During the monsoon, the 
area gets flooded by freshwater from inland regions and saline water from the Gulf of 
Kutch (Ministry of Water Resources, 2014). 
During the site visit (Date: 18th February 2020), we noticed that the terrain is almost flat and 
has distributed bushes. The study area has come under the desert and hot arid climatic zone. 
It has a scarcity of drinking water, and groundwater is highly saline. The entire land is 
identified as barren revenue wasteland. Due to BSF protocols, no phones and cameras are 
allowed after the India bridge; hence, no site photos are available. Therefore, we took only 
elevation readings 
 
	Area Drainage Study for 4.75 GW Wind-Solar Hybrid Project at Rann of Kutch, Gujarat 	Page 4 
at some random locations (Dharmashala, Shakti bet, and Wind masters) of the solar park 
using a handheld Global Positioning System (GPS). 
  
Figure 1: (a) Gujarat state, (b) Kutch district, (c) NTPC plot. 
 
 
 
	Area Drainage Study for 4.75 GW Wind-Solar Hybrid Project at Rann of Kutch, Gujarat 	Page 5 
5. Review of literature 
Kutch is a pre-cratonic western margin rift basin oriented in the East-West direction (Biswas, 
1971). This region belongs to the Mesozoic era and contains the entire stratigraphic record from 
the middle Jurassic to the current-day Holocene (Biswas, 1987). The region of Kutch was 
formed during the eruption of the Reunion Hotspot and is regarded as one of the Continental 
flood basalt volcanic provinces (Besse and Courtillot, 1988). The present-day landscape is 
influenced by the reactivation of the two major faults, the Kutch Mainland Fault and the Katrol 
Hill Fault, during the early Quaternary period (Thakkar et al., 1999). The four main 
physiographic units based on altitude, slope, and variations in relief are (1) The Ranns, (2) The 
Banni plains, (3) The Coastal plains, and (4) The Gulf of Kutch (Thakkar, 2017). Whereas the 
major faults in the area trending East-West are (1) Nagar Parkar Fault, (2) the Allah Bund Fault, 
(3) the Island Belt Fault, (4)  the Banni Fault, (5) the Kutch Mainland Fault, (6) the Katrol hill 
fault and (7) the South Wagad Fault (Mathew et al., 2006). 
The area of interest for the current projects lies in the Great Rann of Kutch, one of the four major 
physiographic units. The Great Rann of Kutch is a unique landscape as it stays water inundated 
for the entirety of the wind-driven monsoon and due to continental fluvial influxes. The Great 
Rann can be considered a Supratidal flat derived from the ancient rivers of Nara and Indus 
(GLENNIE and EVANS, 1976). The Greater Rann is bound by the two E-W trending faults. The 
Nagaur Parker Fault bounds the region from the north, and the Kutch Mainland fault bounds the 
area to the south (Biswas, 1987). Pakistan surrounds the Greater Rann in the North, Rajasthan in 
the North East, The districts of Banaskantha and Mehsana in the East, and the remainder of 
Kutch in the south. The Arabian Sea binds the Kutch district and Surendranagar in the South 
East. 
The history of Kutch is filled with earthquakes of moderate to large intensities. The recent Bhuj 
earthquake of 2001, with a magnitude of 7.7, was the second-largest intraplate earthquake 
known to the world (Mathew et al., 2006). The 1819 earthquake, responsible for the Allah Bund 
fault, a tract of uplifted land, was a7.5 magnitude. The 1819 earthquake claimed over 20,000 
lives in the region of Kutch and its nearby areas. The surface deformation caused by the 1819 
earthquake was due to repetitive earthquakes. The list of major earthquakes compiled up to 1996 
shows magnitudes of 6 and above going back to the sixteenth century (Rajendran and Rajendran, 
2001). 


According to India-WRIS climate statistics, the temperature profile of Kutch in the year range 
1969-2004 shows an increasing trend and has a maximum annual average temperature of 32.54 
ºC. According to Indian Meteorological Department (IMD), the region has shown an increased 
mean maximum temperature of 0.5 ºC over the years, whereas the mean minimum temperature 
has decreased by 0.5 ºC. The annual rainfall is less over the coastal region of Kutch than in the 
land area (Gujarat Ecology Commission, 1997). 
Sea surface temperature has also increased in the last 50 years. A study based on the intensity of 
coral bleaching in the region of the Gulf of Kutch has shown that the reef area has warmed at the 
rate of 0.06 ºC, from 26.04 ºC in 1985 to 26.10 ºC in 2005, while the maximum and minimum 
sea surface temperature remained at 28.9 ºC and 23 ºC, respectively (Vivekanandan et al., 2008). 
The arid nature of the fluvial system of Kutch is influenced by the Last Glacial Maximum 
(LGM) period with strong monsoon conditions in the period the last 13,000 years to 6000 years. 
The period of the previous 3000 to 6000 years is influenced by fluctuating climatic conditions, 
and the sea level was also high by 2m. The sea level dropped to its current state around 3000 
years ago, and the present-day Kutch was formed. (Das et al., 2017). 
The major rivers flowing in the Kutch can be subdivided into Luni lower sub-basin and 
independent drainage network of Runn sub-basin that flows into the Arabian Sea and Gulf 
region (Ministry of Water Resources, 2014). The Luni lower sub-basin is the set of independent 
rivers, and the Luni river drains into the Rann of Kutch. The independent rivers draining into 
Greater Ranns are Bhuki, Bharud, Nara, and Kharod. These independent rivers are seasonal and 
often stay dry throughout the summer months. The rivers originate south of the Katrol hill 
regions that flow into the Gulf, and the Arabian sea is Mitti and Naira rivers. Ephemeral rivers 
also originate in the northern Katrol hill and flow north, cross-cutting the Kutch Mainland Fault 
and draining into the Greater Rann of Kutch. These rivers are the Khari River, Pat River, 
Kaswali River, Pur River, and Khirsara River. These rivers get affected by the intra-tectonic 
activity occurring in the Kutch region (Mathew et al., 2006). 
 
6. Datasets, Model, and Method 
6.1 Datasets 
6.1.1 Topography Sheet 
We obtained topography sheets (No: G42V7, G42V8, G4211, G42V12, and F42D9) from the 
Survey of India and digitized the toposheet maps to identify features over the study area. The 
contours and other salient features are classified in the toposheet map for security reasons. 
However, Rann (salt waste-dry), open scrubs, and roads are marked at a 1:50,000 scale (Figure 
2). 
  
Figure 2: Digitization of features in the Toposheets of the study area. 
 
6.1.2 Digital Elevation Model 
We prepared a digital elevation model (DEM) at 50m resolution over NTPC plots using total 
station survey data (obtained from NTPC). We prepared an elevation-area histogram for the 
NTPC plot (Figure 3). We find that the mean sea level elevation varies from 1.58-to 9.2 m and 
an average of 4.22 m at the NTPC plot. Moreover, we estimated subplot-wise mean, 1, and 99 
percentile elevation values (Table 1). We find that the Southern plot has a low elevation than the 
Northern plot (Figures 4a and 4b). We used Cartosat Digital Elevation Model (CartoDEM 
V3R1). It is a National DEM developed by the Indian Space Research Organization (ISRO). It 
has a 30m fine resolution. CartoDEM products are beneficial in contour generation, drainage 
network analysis; design of hydraulic structures; watershed planning, etc. The CartoDEM uses 
the WGS84 datum, which is based on an ellipsoid. Hence to obtain mean sea level elevation, we 
added (48m) geoid undulation to CartoDEM. CartoDEM is one of the finest DEMs and works 
more accurately over plain and moderately undulating lands (Agarwal et al., 2020; Rajasekhar et 
al., 2018). However, the CartoDEM data has uncertainties. Therefore, we used DEM derived 
through the total station to design the drainage network within the NTPC plant. In contrast, DEM 
prepared by CartoDEM has been used for the study area's catchment demarcation (watershed 
delineation). 
 


  
Figure 3: Histogram of mean sea level elevation vs. area over NTPC plot. 
 
  
Figure 4a: Digital Elevation Model for NTPC plot. 
 
 
  
Figure 4b: Elevation Contour at 0.25m interval for NTPC plot.

Percentile: 
Percentile value is the value below which a given percentage of observations fall. For 
example, 1 percentile value indicates a value below which 1% of other observations lie. 
Same way 99 percentile value indicates a value below which 99% of other observations 
lie. 
 
 
6.1.3 Land Use Land Cover 
Land Use Land Cover (LULC) map provides information on the current landscape. We obtained 
the LULC map for 2016 from the National Remote Sensing Centre (NRSC, 2006) (NRSC). It has 
a 56m resolution. The LULC data sets were derived from multi-temporal Advanced Wide Field 
Sensor (AWiFS) data. The LULC classified in the study area is Rann (Figure 5). It is an 
extensive salt marsh. It spreads between the Gulf of Kutch and the Indus River delta. 
 
  
Figure 5: Land Use Land Cover map for NTPC plot. 
6.1.4 Soil 
To obtain the soil information of the study area, we used Harmonized World Soil Database 
(HWSD), version 1.2 (Figure 6). It has a 1km resolution. Over 16000 different soil mapping 
units were linked in the HWSD. We find saline desert soil (Zg - Gleyic Solonchaks) and high 
calcium desert soil (Xh - Haplic Xerosols) over the study area. Gleyic Solonchaks and Haplic 
Xeosols are founded where the water table is at shallow depth and strongly externally saline. 
These are soils of the deserts with low levels of organic matter. They are eroded mainly by wind 
and have a high soluble salts concentration. 
  
Figure 6: Soil map for NTPC plot. 
 
6.1.5 Rainfall 
We obtained long-term observed daily rainfall from 1951-to 2020 from the India Meteorological 
Department (IMD) for the Kutch district. The daily gridded rainfall has a 0.25º spatial resolution. 
We selected the nearest grid (Latitude: 24.125 degrees and Longitude: 69.625 degrees) data from 
the NTPC plot for rainfall analysis. There is no nearby weather station, so we used IMD gridded 
data. It has been widely used for flood, drought, and hydrology assessments over India (Mishra 
and Shah, 2018; Shah and Mishra, 2016, 2014). 
6.1.6 Air Temperature 
We obtained IMD gridded daily maximum and minimum temperature data from 1951 to 2020. 
The dataset was developed by Srivastava et al. (2009) and is based on 395 observational stations 
across India. Daily maximum and minimum temperatures were regridded to 0.25 degrees (which 
is consistent with the resolution of rainfall) using a lapse rate and digital elevation model (DEM) 
as described by Maurer et al. (2002). 
6.1.7 Wind 
We obtained hourly U and V components of wind at 10 m above ground level between 1980 and 
2020 from ERA5 reanalysis data (Hersbach et al., 2018). It has a 0.25-degree spatial resolution. 
ERA5 is the fifth generation European Centre for Medium-range Weather Forecasts (ECMWF) 
reanalysis for the global climate and weather. Reanalysis combines model data with observations 
from across the world. ERA5 provides hourly estimates for many atmospheric, ocean-wave, and 
land-surface quantities. We also obtained wind gusts 10 m above ground level to estimate 
maximum wind speed over the study area. The ECMWF simulates a maximum 3-second wind at 
10 m height as defined by World Meteorological Organization. The 3-second wind gust is 
computed every step, and the maximum is kept since the last postprocessing. 
6.1.8 Sea Level 
The rise in global mean sea level in recent decades has been one of the significant consequences 
of climate change. Sea-level flooding is a greater risk because the NTPC plot is close to the 
Arabian sea. Therefore, it is essential to understand the change in sea level over the study area. 
We obtained daily sea surface anomaly data at 0.25-degree resolution from Copernicus Climate 
Change Service (C3S). The sea level anomaly is the water height above the mean sea surface in 
a given time and region. It is computed for the twenty-year mean reference period using satellite 
altimetry measurements. The C3S sea level data set is used to produce Ocean Monitoring 
Indicators. Over the study region, we obtained sea-level anomaly data from the 1993-2020 
period. 
As C3S provides sea level anomaly only, we used 50 years return period of extreme sea level 
data from Muis et al. (2016). We used Global Tide and Surge Reanalysis (GTSR) data. It covers 
the entire world's coastline, considers tides and surges, and estimates extreme sea levels. It has 
good agreement with observed sea levels, and the performance of GTSR is similar to many 
regional hydrodynamic models. 
6.1.9 Satellite images 
We carried out flood inundation analysis using satellite images. We obtained sentinel two images 
during the monsoon season of 2019 (Date: 18/09/2019) and 2020 (Date: 22/09/2020). The 
European Space Agency has operated Sentinel-2. The mission is a constellation with two twin 
satellites, Sentinel-2A and Sentinel-2B. The satellites use the multispectral instrument (MSI) to 
analyze different aspects of the Earth-like water, vegetation, and so on using 13 electromagnetic 
spectrum bands. We used Band 3 (Green, 10 m resolution) and Band 12 (SWIR, 20 m 
resolution) to prepare flood inundation maps. The satellite image of the NTPC plot on 14th 
February 2021 is shown in Figure 7. This satellite image is used to identify water extended on 
sunny days.  
  
Figure 7: RGB image of sentinel 2 of NTPC plot (14/02/2021). 
 
6.1.10 Projected Climate 
To understand the projected change in climatic variables (rainfall and temperature) under 
changing climate scenarios, we selected the latest General Circulation Model / Global Climate 
Model (GCM) from the Coupled Model Intercomparison Project phase 5 (CMIP5) GCMs for the 
period 1975-2074 under Representative Concentration Pathways (RCP) 4.5 and 8.5. The RCP 
4.5 assumes a low-emission scenario in which radiative forcing is projected to increase by 4.5 
W/m2 by the end of the 21st century. On the other hand, an increase of 8.5 W/m2 is projected 
under the high emission scenario of RCP 8.5 by the end of the 21st century. The 
Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) fifth assessment report featured climate 
models from CMIP5. Based on the previous studies (Aadhar and Mishra, 2019; Ashfaq et al., 
2017), we selected the five best CMIP5-GCMs with better monsoon representation and less bias 
during the observed climate. More information on GCMs is available in Table 2. We bias-
corrected daily precipitation and temperature data from the five GCMs and two RCPs using the 
methodology of Hempel et al. 
(2013).

6.2 Numerical Modelling 
The present study uses the hydrological model (SWAT) and hydraulic model (HEC-RAS) as the 
numerical modeling tools. 
6.2.1 Hydrological Model 
The Soil Water Assessment Tool (SWAT) is a semi-distributed, physically-based hydrological 
model. It has been widely used to simulate daily streamflow (Arnold et al., 1998; Gosain et al., 
2006; Mishra and Lilhare, 2016). The SWAT simulates the water budget (runoff and 
evapotranspiration). The SWAT model processes are presented in Figure 8. The model 
discretizes watershed into sub-basin and then to unique land use/soil/slope attribute. The SWAT 
requires meteorological, land use, soil, and slope information to simulate the water budget. We 
simulated daily streamflow at different micro-catchment outlets of the NTPC plot. 
  
Figure 8: Schematic of hydrologic cycle presented in SWAT (Source: Arnold et al., 1998). 
 
6.2.2 Hydraulic Model 
We used Hydrologic Engineering Centre-River Analysis System (HEC-RAS) to simulate flood 
propagation and prepare the inundation maps. The HEC-RAS is a hydraulic model developed by 
the US Army Corps of Engineers. The model contains various river analysis components, 
including the spatial mapping of water surface elevation and water depth. The model solves 2D 
Saint-Venant equations. The HEC-RAS model has been widely used to map floodplains and 
simulate flood elevations (Parhi, 2018; Patel and Gundaliya, 2016). The SWAT model's 
discharge hydrograph (output) has been used as input in HEC-RAS. This report focused on the 
flood inundation mapping over the NTPC plant using the HEC-RAS model. 
6.3 Methods of Analysis 
6.3.1 Analysis of rainfall 
We estimated mean annual rainfall, maximum rainfall, and the number of rainy days (Rainfall 
>=1 mm) using IMD daily rainfall data over the Kutch region from 1951 to 2020. We also 
analyzed the time series (1951-2020) of annual rainfall, maximum rainfall, and rainy days for the 
nearest grid of the NTPC site. We estimated the mean monthly rainfall at the NTPC plot. 
6.3.2 Analysis of air temperature 
We estimated annual mean maximum and minimum temperature, 1day maximum temperature, 
1day minimum temperature, number of hot (maximum temperature > 40 ºC) and cold (minimum 
temperature < 10 ºC) days using IMD daily temperature data over the Kutch region from 1951 to 
2020. We analyzed the annual time series of annual mean maximum/minimum temperature, 1-
day maximum/minimum temperature, and a number of hot/cold days for the nearest grid of the 
NTPC site. We also estimated the mean monthly average temperature at the NTPC plot. 
6.3.3 Analysis of wind 
We estimated daily wind speed and direction by converting hourly data into daily data. We used 
the below formula to estimate wind speed and meteorological wind direction. 
 
	Wind speed (m/s) = √𝑈	+ 𝑉 
Wind direction (degree) = 57.29578* (𝑎𝑡𝑎𝑛2(𝑈, 𝑉)) + 180 
Here, U is the eastward wind speed, and V is the northward wind speed at 10 m above ground. 
We also estimated the annual mean wind speed, maximum wind speed, and the number of windy 
days (wind speed > 6 m/s) over the Kutch district. Moreover, we estimated the annual time series 
of mean wind speed, maximum wind speed, and the number of windy days for the nearest grid of 
the NTPC site. We used wind gust data to estimate maximum wind speed. We calculated the 
mean monthly wind speed and direction (degree) at the NTPC plot. 
6.3.4 Analysis of sea level 
We analyzed sea level anomaly by calculating maximum sea-level rise over the Arabian sea 
using C3S data. We also estimated the annual maximum sea-level rise by taking the area average 
from 1993-to 2020. 
6.3.5 Flood inundation maps using Satellite images 
We prepared flood inundation maps using sentinel two bands during the monsoon season of 2019 
and 2020. We selected cloud cover with less than 5% images and estimated Modified 
Normalized Difference Water Index (MNDWI). It uses Green and SWIR bands to enhance open 
water features (Du et al., 2016; Xu, 2006). It ranges between -1 to 1, where greater than 0 
indicates the presence of water. The formula for MNDWI calculation is given below. 
(𝐺𝑟𝑒𝑒𝑛 − 𝑆𝑊𝐼𝑅)
𝑀𝑁𝐷𝑊𝐼 =   
(𝐺𝑟𝑒𝑒𝑛 + 𝑆𝑊𝐼𝑅)
6.3.6 Delineation of watershed 
We delineated a micro watershed over the NTPC plant and a macro watershed using total 
stationderived DEM and CartoDEM, respectively. We filled the void in DEM and then estimated 
flow direction, flow accumulation, and stream order to delineate the watershed. Watershed 
delineation helps to derive stream, stream network, and catchment area. 
6.3.7 Simulation of streamflow 
We simulated daily discharge at different outlets using the SWAT model. As observed discharge 
data is unavailable, we considered curve number (CN) 98 based on soil and land use conditions 
(Neitsch et al., 2009). The surface runoff is computed using the modified SCS curve number 
method (Soil Conservation Service Engineering Division, 1972). The SCS curve number 
equation is 
	(𝑅	− 0.2𝑆𝑝)
	𝑄	=   
	(𝑅	+ 0.8𝑆𝑝)
𝑆𝑝   
𝐶𝑁
Where Qsurf is the runoff, Rday is the rainfall depth for the day, Sp is the retention parameter. 
6.3.8 Estimation of the return period 
The Generalized Extreme Value (GEV) distribution was fitted to estimate the return level. It has 
three parameters: location, scale, and shape for the time series. We estimated rainfall, 
minimum/maximum temperature, wind speed, and flood magnitude return period for 2, 5, 10, 25, 
50, and 100 years over the NTPC plant. We estimated rainfall intensity over the study area using 
the general equation given in Indian Roads Congress (2013).  
Io = (F/T) ((T+1)/ (t+1)) = F/2(1+1/T) 
Where,  
Io= One- hour rainfall intensity in mm/hr  
F= Total precipitation in T hours 
T=Duration of Storm in hour  
t =Smaller Duration of Storm in hour  
 
One-hour rainfall intensity for different return periods is calculated using the above formula. 
Generally, the rainfall intensity will decrease as it increases in duration. 
The extreme sea levels corresponding to the 25 and 50 years return period were analyzed using 
the GTSR sea level data for 1979-2014 and fitted a Gumbel distribution using the 
maximumlikelihood method. 
6.3.9 Analysis of Projected Climate 
Using ensemble mean of GCMs data, we estimated projected change in climatic variables 
(rainfall and temperature) for different reference periods (2025-2049 and 2050-2074) against the 
base period (1975-1999) under different RCPs (RCP 4.5 and RCP 8.5) scenarios. We estimated 
the projected percentage change in mean annual and extreme rainfall (99th percentile of rainy 
days). Moreover, we calculated the projected absolute change in mean annual air temperature 
and maximum temperature using the below formulas. 
Projected percentage change =   *100 
Projected absolute change = (𝑅𝑒𝑓𝑒𝑟𝑒𝑛𝑐𝑒 𝑃𝑒𝑟𝑖𝑜𝑑 − 𝐵𝑎𝑠𝑒 𝑃𝑒𝑟𝑖𝑜𝑑) 
6.3.10 Proposed drainage network design 
Based on two year return period maximum discharge within subplots (CPHEEO, 2019), we 
designed internal and peripheral rectangular (Figure 9a) brick lining drains as per IS 10430 
(2000); IS 3873 (1993); IS 4515 (2002), and IS 456 (2000) to minimize waterlogging over the 
NTPC plant. 
The design assumes that the flow is a uniform open channel flow using the Manning Equation. 
	𝑉 = 	 𝑆 
𝑛
Here, V is the average velocity, n is the Manning roughness coefficient, R = A/P is the hydraulic 
radius, and S is the bed slope. We used the below formula to estimate other properties of the 
drains. 
Rectangle drain 
Cross sectional area (A) = B * D; 
Wetted perimeter (P) = B + (2 * D); 
Here: B -Width, and D - Depth 
 
Figure 9a: Schematic diagram of the rectangular drain. 
 
6.3.11 Proposed recharge pits 
We proposed recharge pits to divert surface runoff to groundwater over the NTPC plot. We 
proposed recharge pits at the low-lying area in each subplot. The below steps are considered 
while constructing the recharge pit ((P. K. Shrivastava, 2013) and Figure 9b). 
1.	The diameter of the bore is 250 mm, at the lowest point of depression. The bore depth should 
be based on the groundwater table and strata. 
2.	Insertion of 4 (four) strainer pipes (200 mm size) and inserting gravel between the pipe and 
the bore. 
3.	Strainer pipe to be kept 0.60 m above the ground surface to prevent direct entry of water or 
any other object. 
4.	Strainer to be capped at the top to prevent any accident. 
5.	Digging a pit of size 10.0 m x 10.0 m x 2.0 m around the bore. 
6.	Filling the pit by half-baked broken bricks till 0.50 m below ground surface to provide 
sufficient free board for water to stand in the pit. 
7.	Construct a bund around the pit with a provision to allow the water to come into it from the 
watershed area. 
8.	Galvanized wire net may be provided at the entry of the pit, preventing any organic impurity 
from entering the pit. 
  
Figure 9b: Schematic of recharge pit (Source: (P. K. Shrivastava, 2013)) 
  
7. Results and discussion 
7.1 Rainfall 
First, we estimated annual rainfall, maximum rainfall, and the number of rainy days from 1951 - 
to 2020 over the Kutch district and NTPC plot (Figure 10 and Table 3). The mean annual rainfall 
varies between 234 and 577 mm (Figure 10a), whereas the 1-day maximum rainfall magnitude 
varies between 107 and 368 mm (Figure 10b). The mean annual number of rainy days varies 
between 12 and 45 days in the Kutch district (Figure 10c). We also analyzed the time series of 
rainfall data over the NTPC plot. Annual rainfall varies between 1 - 996 mm from 1951-to 2020 
(Figure 10d). Whereas annual maximum rainfall varies from 0.5 mm to 242 mm (Figure 10e), 
the number of rainy days varies from 0 - to 73 (Figure 10f). The mean annual rainfall is 390 mm 
over the NTPC plot. Dave and James (2017) and Mohanty et al. (2015) found that monsoon 
rainfall increased over the Kutch region during the 2004-2013 period.

We estimated the mean monthly rainfall over the NTPC plot from 1951 - to 2020 (Figure 11). 
Usually, the rainfall occurs during the May-October months. However, the main contribution 
comes during the July-September months.

7.2 Air Temperature 
Second, we analyzed the maximum and minimum temperatures. We estimated the mean annual 
maximum temperature, 1-day maximum temperature, and the number of hot days over the Kutch 
district and the NTPC area between 1951-and 2020 (Figure 12). We find mean annual maximum 
temperature varies between 31- 34 ºC. Whereas mean annual 1-day maximum temperature and 
number of hot days vary between 40- 48 ºC and 0-52, respectively, in the Kutch district. We 
performed a timer annual time series analysis at the NTPC site and found that the mean annual 
maximum temperature varies between 31.5-33.5 ºC (Table 4). Moreover, the annual maximum 
temperature and hot days vary between 37-42 and 0-16, respectively. Ray et al. (2008) found a 
significantly steady temperature increase over the Kutch region. 
  
Figure 12: (a) mean annual maximum temperature, (b) maximum temperature, (c) mean annual 
number of hot (maximum temperature > 40 ºC) days over Kutch district estimated during 1951-
2020. Time series of (d) mean annual maximum temperature, (e) annual maximum temperature, 
and (f) annual hot days over NTPC plot.

We estimated the mean annual minimum temperature, mean annual 1-day minimum temperature 
and mean the annual number of cold (minimum temperature < 10 ºC) days over the Kutch 
district (Figure 13). We find mean annual minimum temperature varies between 19.5-21.5 ºC.

minimum temperature, and the number of cold days over the NTPC plot (Table 5). The mean 
annual minimum temperature varies between 20-22 ºC. Moreover, the 1-day annual minimum

plot.

We also estimated the mean monthly air temperature by taking the mean maximum and 
minimum temperature of the NTPC plot area during the 1951-2020 period (Figure 14). We find 
that the mean air temperature varies between 19.6-31.9 ºC. During May-June, we find the 
highest mean air temperature. The mean air temperature is 27 ºC over the NTPC site. 
  
Figure 14: Mean monthly air temperature over NTPC plot during 1951-2020. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
7.3 Wind 
We estimated the mean annual wind speed and direction at 10 m height above ground level over 
the Kutch district and NTPC site using ERA5 reanalysis data (Figure 15). We find that the mean 
annual wind speed varies between 3-5.5 m/s over the Kutch district and the wind direction is 
mostly Northeast and East. We estimated maximum wind speed using wind gust data at 10 m 
above ground. We find that maximum wind gust varies between 25-40 m/s. We also find that the 
mean annual number of windy days (wind speed > 6 m/s) varies between 26 and 156. Moreover, 
the annual average wind speed varies between 4 and 5 m/s from 1980 to 2020 (Table 6). We also 
find that the annual maximum wind gust and windy days vary between 18-32 m/s and 60-119, 
respectively. Draxl et al. (2014) and IRENA-GWEC (2013) found that the highest wind speed 
(annual mean wind speed 8t m/s at 80 m height above ground level) over the Gulf of Kutch. 
  
Figure 15: (a) mean annual wind speed, (b) maximum wind gust, (c) mean annual number of 
windy (wind speed > 6 m/s) days over Kutch district estimated during 1980-2020. Time series of 
(d) mean annual wind speed, (e) annual maximum wind speed, and (f) annual windy days over 
the NTPC plot. The red arrow shows the wind direction.

We also estimated the mean monthly wind speed and direction over the NTPC plot area from 
1980 to 2020 (Figure 16). We find mean monthly wind speed varies between 2.8-6.6 m/s, and 
wind direction varies between 126-251 degrees. The mean annual wind speed is 4.6 m/s at 10 m 
above ground level. 
  
Figure 16: Mean monthly wind speed and direction over NTPC plot during 1980-2020. The red 
arrow shows the wind direction. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
7.4 Sea Level 
We analyzed maximum sea-level change over the Arabian sea using daily sea level anomaly data 
between 1993 and 2020 (Figure 17). We find maximum sea-level rise by 0.5 m. We also find 
that the average sea level rose by 0.28 m in 2019 (Table 7). Overall, results show an increase in 
the trend in sea level rise over the Arabian sea. A recent report from Gujarat Ecological 
Education and Research Foundation (2017) and the Ministry of Environment & Forests (2012) 
found that sealevel rise along the Gulf of Kutch to be 0.4-2 mm/year. 
  
Figure 17: (a) Maximum sea level anomaly (m) and (b) annual area average sea level rise (m) 
over the Arabian sea for the period of 1993-2020.
